# 1100\n
{Oxford, Jesus College 29, part II, English on fols. 144r-195r; 198r-200v}\n
C13b2\n
372 244 W\n
{=E Herefords=}\n
{=Sample tagged: fols. 156r-168v, 169r-174v, 179v-180v, 182r-185v, 187r-188v: The Owl and\n
the Nightingale, Poema Morale, Death\'s Wither Clench, Orison to Our Lady,\n
Doomsday, The Latemest Day, the Ten Abuses, Lutel Soth Sermun, A Luue Ron=}\n
{=Script - proto-Gothic, informal, amateur book hand=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Note that he final stroke of some letters, e.g. G and E, is drawn out\n
and sometimes the scribe rests the pen making a dot. This, with the\n
end of line punctus could be read as colon but I have chosen to take\n
it as unintentional, punctus and punctus elevatus being the only\n
punctuation marks used=}\n

initial letters and the following letters of the next four lines.\n

not naturally follow the indirect article ONE and OE dael "dale" is\n
normally neuter so should not attract a feminine adjective=}\n

$/T<pr_yE\n
$marsh/n<pr{rh}_MerSCHE\n
{=Although "worse" and "marsh" do not provide a true rhyme, "marsh" is\n
confirmed by both J and C versions and is likely therefore to have been\n
the reading in the common exemplar. Whether it was the reading of the\n
original is not knowable, but as far as semantics are concerned, I\n
think it unnecessary to propose an otherwise unattested ME word from\n
an unattested OE *mae:rs "field boundaries" for this word (see Cartlidge\n
2001:114 following Kristensson and ultimately Stanley (for refs. see\n
Cartlidge). OE mersc is applied not only to permanent wetlands but also\n
to tidal marshes and to flood meadows that are not always inundated.\n
HAUECK is an unspecified "hawk" and could well imply a marsh harrier,\n
hen harrier or common buzzard all of which are frequently to be found\n
hunting over meadow and marshland. Sparrow hawk, Montagu\'s harrier and\n
peregrine falcon are also possibilities. Crows are to be found\n
anywhere where there are food sources for them, which would include\n
not only any kind of carrion to be found in marsh or meadow but also\n
the eggs and nestlings of meadow-nesting and  marsh-nesting birds.\n
They would be just as likely to mob a hawk "by the marsh" as anywhere else=}\n
{.}\n

$tongue/nOd_TUNGE\n
{=Presumably with the sense "prick, male organ". Most editors read\n
IS TUNGE as ISTUNGE, past participle of "sting" with the sense\n
"penetrated". This reading is based on a correction of the Cotton\n
version (also IS TUNGE) in a later hand which adds S between the\n
two words to make ISSTUNGE. It seems to me that ISSTUNGE, with double\n
S, is not itself a plausible form for the past participle. In fact it\n
seems to me more likely that the corrector was trying to change one\n
noun ("tongue") to another ("sting"). Cartlidge (2001) reports the\n
MS readings accurately but accepts the reading ISTUNGE "stung". Only\n
Wells (1906) suggests the possibility of reading "tongue" for\n
"pudendum". He also surmises that the common original had either\n
IS TUNGE ("his tongue") or ISTUNGE ("stung"). Whatever the author\'s\n
intention, here it is the scribal text that must be interpreted and\n
tagged accordingly=}\n
$under/pr_VNDER\n
$ga:ra/n<pr{rh}_GORE\n

$in{c}/pr_ON\n
$thin?/n<pr{rh}_yINNE\n
{="in straits, having a thin time?", see Stanley (1960). The Cotton\n
version also has yINNE with clearly written thorn, but in that scribe\'s\n
script the differentiation between thorn and wynn is not at all\n
consistent and in Cotton it would be perfectly reasonable to read wINNE.\n
The editors, including Stanley, do so. The translation would then be\n
something like "when the heart is in strife, agitated". Against this\n
reading in Cotton is the Jesus reading. The J scribe does not use a\n
separate wynn rune but on the few occasions when he fails to alter\n
exemplar wynn to W he adopts a Y-shaped dotted character not a thorn.\n
His use of thorn here suggests that the exemplar had clear thorn not\n
wynn. In any case the J scribe seems to have intended the sequence\n
yINNE here=}\n
{.}\n

$fox/n_FOR\n
{=sic - error for FOX. Cotton version also has FOR. The mistake\n
probably arises from the fact that in some formal scripts the first\n
element of X is identical to that of R and the second stroke, crossing\n
the first obliquely from top right to bottom left, can also be identical\n
at its top end to the limb of R. If the cross-stroke is kept short, without\n
an extravagant descender, the two figurae can look very similar indeed. It\n
is interesting that in the case of FOR for "fox" both the J and the C\n
scribe substituted 2-shaped R, usual in their scripts after O, for what\n
is likely to have been X mistaken for the straight short R used in other contexts=}\n

$te:am?/n_TEM\n
{="penalty?" This is clearly a misreading by the J scribe presumably of exemplar\n
(H)AREM - the Cotton version has HAREM - but it may well have had some\n
meaning to the J scribe who often adapts the text where he strikes\n
difficulties. The sense demands something like "harm" or "difficulty" or\n
"loss". The OE legal term te:am had developed a number of senses by the\n
ME period, see MED s.v. te:m(e n.(1). The sense given under 2. b is:\n
"the penalty for not producing a warrantor in a suit over disputed\n
property" is very specific and its use by the J scribe here would\n
require a generalisation in sense from the specific legal usage. I offer\n
it as a possibility, however, because the use of legal terminology is\n
part of the fabric of the poem=}\n

{=Note that FOR yURRE may be a misreading of some form of "further" in\n
the exemplar. Cotton has FOR yURE with only a very slight gap between\n
FOR and yURE and it seems reasonable to read "further" in Cotton as all\n
editors have done. In J the gap between the two words is very clear and\n
the J scribe appears to have meant something other than "further" here.\n

definitie article often elides with following words beginning with a vowel=}\n

which mention is made of \'beate uirginis tuae aetheldrithe\'". There\n
follow the Rules of St Benedict and of St Augustine=}\n

$/P12N_yU\n
$give/vps12+P_*IU+ES+TU $/vps12+P_+ES+TU $/P12N-as_+TU\n
{=Onions thinks this is for "thou give us to". Grammatically one can see the\n
point, because an imperative is required and the +TU is redundant anyway\n
with the preceding yU. But formally this explanation is completely\n
implausible because it requires one to take +ES for "us" and +TU for\n
post-poned "to" - neither spelling being phonetically explicable nor\n
paralleled. *IUESTU is fine for \'thou givest\'. MB thinks (and so do I)\n
that it\'s more likely that the scribe has switched in mid-stream from an\n
imperative "thou give" to a present "givest thou". Cf. "forgive us thou"\n
below with the +TU crossed through to make the imperative good=}\n
{.}\n

$/RTA_yer-E\n
$all/av_AL\n
$guilt/vps13-ct_KILT\n
$/P21Oi_US\n
{=MB conjectures (pers com) that AL KILT may be a mistake for contracted\n
AKILT "aguilts". The contracted form is formally acceptable, but AL\n
may simply be a somewhat awkward use of the commonly appearing\n
adverbial "all, entirely". The whole phrase may be a corruption of\n
the more common plural version "as we forgive all those who (have) guilt(ed)\n
(i.e. trespass(ed) against) us". Given the common use of the past tense\n
in other versions of this phrase: "have guilted us", AL could perhaps\n
alternatively be a corruption of some form of "have" and KILT would then\n
have begun life as a past participle. AL could then have begun life as\n
Ay or AT. AS (for HAS) would be an easier error to account for, but -S\n
ending is not likely in Salisbury at this date. Or if it were\n
subjunctive it could possibly be AF which might also be easier to\n
account for palaeographically. I am dependent on MB\'s transcript; he has\n
yer E. But my own feeling would be that yer - i.e. thorn with a hook -\n
is used in the same way as a barred thorn - i.e. for "that" rather than\n
for "there/where" (cf. scribe of Cotton Caligula A.ix (many examples)\n
and hands A (6 exx) and D (2 exx) of Cambridge Trinity College 323. I\n
conjecture that it is also possible if the gap in the MS is not too\n
great (and MB confirms that it isn\'t) that the <E> could be part of\n
the "that" relative pronoun. I haven\'t come across hooked thorn in\n
combination with E for "that" but there is a number of examples of\n
barred thorn in combination with E. e.g. Lambeth Homilies has y~E\n
once for relative pronoun, FORdON-y~E once for the conjunction, and\n
conversely, with bar instead of hook, y~E once for "there" adverb.\n
Hand B of Lambeth 487 (Ureisun of ure lauerde) has y~E once for\n
"thee" and y~ET once for demonstrative "that". The Nero text of\n
Ancrene Riwle has y~E once for the demonstrative and twice for\n
relative pronoun plural and there is one example of y~E demonstrative\n
pronoun in the late 12th cent. text of the Vision of St Edwin in BL\n
Add 15340 from Winchester. I have provisionally therefore tagged the\n
combination yer and E as relative pronoun. I have not tried to emend\n
the text further, although the AL remains awkward=}\n
{.}\n

nevertheless the best reading. It otherwise could perhaps be the verbal\n

imperative of werian, but the ending +ES is then hard to account for=}\n
